a time - for quite a long time, two hundred years. But, in the end, the last
of these kings paid a terrible price for the mistakes made by the kings
who had ruled before. The country where this happened was France.

Rome 的教宗們對反抗他們權威的叛亂當然不高興。他們本想把 Luther 送上宗教裁判所的審判台，本想把他燒死在火刑柱上，如果 Luther 在德國沒有那麼多的追隨者的話。在德國，教宗沒有足夠的權力來傷害 Luther，但在義大利，教宗有權力對抗這種新精神。他們在 Florence 燒死了修士 Savonarola，因為他竟敢稱邪惡的教宗 Alexander Borgia 為「魔鬼之子」。他們燒死了另一位修士 Giordano Bruno，因為他竟敢說地球是宇宙中的一顆行星，繞著太陽運行。而 Galileo Galilei 只是因為否認了他所相信的關於地球的真理——地球繞太陽運行——才逃脫了酷刑和死亡。在義大利，新精神——宗教改革的精神、發現和科學的精神——被壓制了。在西班牙，教宗找到了國王們作為他們心甘情願的幫手——還記得西班牙的 Philip 如何試圖以武力為 Rome 教會征服荷蘭和英格蘭，卻慘遭失敗。現在我們來到另一個歐洲國家，那裡的國王們也竭盡全力阻止進步的步伐、把時鐘倒轉、以「神聖權利」統治。他們成功了一段時間——相當長的時間，兩百年。但最終，這些國王中的最後一位為前任國王所犯的錯誤付出了可怕的代價。這件事發生的國家就是法國。
France, the land of Charlemagne, the land of Joan of Arc, had for
many centuries been a land that was devoutly Roman Catholic, and obedient to the Pope. The Knights of France were the first knights who followed the Pope's call for a Crusade against the Turks. And it was also in
France that the first and more beautiful Gothic churches with pointed
arches and stained-glass windows had been built.
But France was also the country where the feudal system was harsher
and lasted longer than in most other countries. In France for a very long
time the peasants remained serfs who could be bought and sold with

法國，Charlemagne 的國度，Joan of Arc 的國度，幾個世紀以來一直是一個虔誠信奉天主教、服從教宗的土地。法國騎士是第一批響應教宗號召去對抗突厥人的十字軍東征的騎士。法國也是第一批更美麗的哥德式教堂——帶有尖拱和彩色玻璃窗——被建造的地方。但法國也是封建制度比大多數其他國家更加嚴酷、持續更久的國家。在法國很長一段時間裡，農民一直是農奴，可以隨著
the land like the trees that grew on it. And in France, too, the knights and
noblemen were under strict obedience to the king. In France there was
no Magna Carta, which set out what a king was and was not allowed to
do. In France the will of the King was the law, and the King had only one
master above him: the Pope in Rome. That's how it was in France, and
that's how the kings of that country wanted it to stay forever.
And then came the time of the Reformation. John Calvin who was a
Frenchman had to flee from France to Switzerland. But from Geneva his
teaching spread to France, and many thousands of Frenchmen became
Protestants, or, as they were called in France, Huguenots. There were so

many of these Huguenots, even amongst the noblemen and lords, that it
seemed as if the King of France, whether he liked it or not, could do
nothing about it. In fact, it seemed that in France
Protestants and
Catholics would live in peace together. But it only seemed so. The King at
that time (Charles IX) was a weak person who left the government entirely in the hands of his mother, an Italian Princess, Catherine de Medici.
Catherine de Medici was
quite determined to destroy the French
Protestants, the Huguenots, but not by an open attack. This cunning
queen knew that if it came to an open, armed conflict between Catholics
and Protestants, it might take years and the whole country would be ruined by civil war until the Protestants were suppressed. Instead of this,
Catherine de Medici and her advisers, planned to destroy the Huguenots
with one blow that should come down on them without warning.
In great secrecy all the Roman Catholics were informed that on the
day of St Bartholomew, August 24, at night, all people known to be
Huguenots were to be wiped out, and it was the duty of every good
Catholic to do his share of the killing. And - strangely enough - the secret was kept; the Protestants heard no whisper and went peacefully
about their business and talked with their Catholic neighbours without
any idea what was planned for them.
A few days before chalk-marks were made on the houses where
Huguenots lived, but the Huguenots hardly noticed these marks and
gave no thought to them.
The day of St Bartholomew, August 24, 1572, passed quietly, but
when darkness fell a signal from the Royal Palace in Paris was given, and
parties of armed soldiers started the horrors of this night. They stormed
into the houses marked with chalk and killed every man, woman and
child they found. And once the soldiers had started the massacre, the
Roman Catholic citizens armed themselves and joined in and fell on their
unsuspecting Protestant neighbours. It was one of the worst massacres
in history.
Most of the killing took place in Paris where many Huguenots lived.
But there were also other parts of France where these horrible murders
took place. Altogether, thirty thousand Protestants were killed in this
one night. The event is called the Massacre of St Bartholomew's Night.
A few thousand Huguenots escaped to England where they were well
:
:

received
and helped. Many of these Huguenot refugees brought new
skills to Britain and so repaid the kindness shown to them.
But, in France, Catherine de Medici, had achieved what she wanted
By wiping out the Protestants, she had not only earned the gratitude of
the Pope, she had also made sure that for a long time to come, that people in France would not dare to challenge the authority of either Pope or
King. The Massacre of St Bartholomew's Night had kept the new spirit
out of France - at least for a time.

大部分屠殺發生在許多 Huguenot 新教徒居住的 Paris。但法國其他地方也發生了這些可怕的謀殺。總共有三萬名新教徒在這一夜被殺。這一事件被稱為 St Bartholomew 之夜大屠殺。幾千名 Huguenot 新教徒逃到了英格蘭，在那裡受到了善待和幫助。許多 Huguenot 難民把新的技藝帶到英國，以此回報對他們的善意。但在法國，Catherine de Medici 達到了她的目的。通過消滅新教徒，她不僅贏得了教宗的感激，還確保了在很長一段時間內，法國人民不敢挑戰教宗或國王的權威。St Bartholomew 之夜大屠殺把新精神擋在了法國之外——至少暫時如此。

Chapter 18
Le Roi Soleil

第十八章
太陽王

Catherine de Medici was certainly an evil woman; one can hardly imagine
the kind of soul who planned and quite calmly arranged the massacre of
thousands of innocent people. But, on the other hand, in the time in
which Catherine lived, the killing of people who had a different faith was
quite common. In Britain while the Protestants killed
each other -
Church of England against Covenanters - Catherine was only more cunning in her ways, but otherwise she only did what was being done all
over Europe in the terrible century that followed the Reformation.

Catherine de Medici 無疑是一個邪惡的女人；人們很難想像是怎樣的靈魂能策劃並冷靜地安排對數千名無辜者的屠殺。但另一方面，在 Catherine 生活的那個時代，殺害不同信仰的人是相當普遍的事。在英國，新教徒之間也在互相殘殺——英格蘭國教對抗盟約派——Catherine 只是手段更加狡猾，但她所做的不過是宗教改革之後那個可怕世紀裡全歐洲都在做的事。

Imagine that Catherine were brought before a court of law for the
massacre of St Bartholomew's Night; she could have said in her defence:
"By this one night of bloodshed I saved France from years of civil war in
which far more than thirty thousand would have been killed. Look at the
Thirty Years' War in Germany, look at the Civil War between Roundheads
and Cavaliers in Britain. In Germany and in Britain far more lives were
lost, far more suffering was caused than the deaths and suffering of that
one night in France. Moreover I saved the power of the kings in France.
In Britain the Stuart Kings lost their power, in Germany the Emperor had
to give in to his noblemen and lords. Only in France the power of the
King not only remained, but grew even stronger."

假設 Catherine 因 St Bartholomew 之夜大屠殺被帶上法庭；她可以為自己辯護說：「用這一夜的流血，我使法國免於多年的內戰——那將殺死遠遠超過三萬人。看看德國的三十年戰爭，看看英國圓頭黨與騎士黨之間的內戰。在德國和英國，失去的生命遠多於此，造成的苦難也遠超過法國那一夜的死亡與痛苦。而且我保住了法國國王的權力。在英國，Stuart 國王們失去了權力；在德國，皇帝不得不向貴族和領主屈服。唯有在法國，國王的權力不僅保住了，而且變得更加強大。」

Of course, in reality Catherine could not have spoken like that, for the
Thirty Years' War in Germany, the Civil War in Britain, came years after
her death. But she could and did realize that the old order, the old authority of King and Pope would be overthrown, civil wars would rage -
and the Massacre of St Bartholomew's Night was her way to prevent this
from happening.

當然，在現實中 Catherine 不可能這麼說，因為德國的三十年戰爭、英國的內戰都發生在她死後多年。但她確實意識到，舊秩序、國王和教宗的舊權威將被推翻，內戰將肆虐——而 St Bartholomew 之夜大屠殺就是她阻止這一切發生的方式。

And so, in the following century, while in Britain the power of kings
crumbled and was swept away, in France the power of the King grew
stronger, it became absolute power, as it is called.

因此，在接下來的一個世紀裡，當英國的王權崩解並被掃除時，法國國王的權力卻變得更加強大，成為所謂的絕對權力。

And while Britain and Germany were shaken by civil wars France prospered. While in Britain, King James wrote books and talked about the "divine rights of kings," and James's son, Charles I, lost his life in trying to
rule by "divine right," at the same time in France there was a King who indeed ruled by "divine right" - who ruled with absolute power. This king
was Louis XIV.

當英國和德國被內戰所震撼時，法國卻繁榮昌盛。在英國，James 國王寫書空談「君權神授」，James 的兒子 Charles 一世因試圖以「神聖權利」統治而丟了性命，而在同一時期的法國，卻真有一位國王確實以「神聖權利」統治——以絕對的權力統治。這位國王就是 Louis 十四。

There was a kind of parliament in France, it was called the "Estates
General," but these Estates General could only advise the King, they
could ask him to do this or that, they could ask him not to do this or that,
but they could not interfere with anything he wanted to do. Nothing can
illustrate the whole situation better than the words spoken by Louis XIV
on one occasion.

法國也有一種議會，稱為「三級會議」（Estates General），但這個三級會議只能向國王提建議，他們可以請求國王做這做那，也可以請求他不要做這做那，但無法干預他想做的任何事。沒有什麼比 Louis 十四在某個場合說的話更能說明整個局面了。

It happened eleven years after Charles I had been beheaded in front
of his palace in Britain. At that time, in 1660, Louis XIV, King of France was
only twenty-two years old. However, he already had very expensive
tastes - he wanted to build an enormous new palace for himself. There
was not enough money in the treasury and so he sent a command to his
parliament - to the Estates General - to increase the taxes.

事情發生在 Charles 一世在英國的宮殿前被斬首十一年後。當時是 1660 年，法國國王 Louis 十四才二十二歲。然而他已經有了非常奢侈的品味——他想為自己建造一座巨大的新宮殿。國庫裡沒有足夠的錢，於是他向他的議會——三級會議——下達了增稅的命令。

Having sent a messenger with this request to the assembly, Louis
went hunting, a sport in which he took great pleasure. When he came
back from the hunt he was told that the noblemen of the Estates General
were very unhappy about his demand for making the taxes higher, they
thought the taxes paid by the French people were already too high and
it would mean great hardship to make them still higher. Young King
Louis was furious. He rode in his hunting outfit, as he was, to Paris and to
the building where the assembly had gathered. With his riding-whip in
his hand he entered and strode into the great hall.

派信使帶著這個請求去議會之後，Louis 就去打獵了——他非常喜愛這項運動。打獵回來時，有人告訴他三級會議的貴族們對加稅的要求非常不滿，他們認為法國人民已經繳了太多稅，再加稅將帶來巨大的困苦。年輕的 Louis 國王勃然大怒。他就穿著獵裝騎馬前往 Paris，來到議會聚集的大廳。手持馬鞭，他走了進去，大步跨入大廳。

All the members of the Estates General stood up as His Majesty, a
youngster of twenty-two, walked in. Louis looked
at them contemptuously and said, "What is all this nonsense about the taxes? I need the
money."

當這位二十二歲的年輕陛下走進來時，三級會議的所有成員都站了起來。Louis 輕蔑地看著他們說：「關於稅收的這些廢話是怎麼回事？我需要錢。」

One of the members replied, "Most Gracious Majesty, forgive us for
speaking freely but we all here are in agreement that it is not good for
the state if so much money is to be spent on a new palace, the state cannot afford to waste money on such unnecessary luxuries, we must consider the state of France as a whole, we must have a regard for the state
first and then ..."

其中一位成員回答：「最仁慈的陛下，請寬恕我們直言，但我們在場所有人一致認為，如果把這麼多錢花在新宮殿上，對國家不利。國家承受不起把錢浪費在這種不必要的奢侈品上，我們必須考慮法國整體的國情，我們必須首先考慮國家，然後再......」

"The state, the state," shouted Louis furiously, and he hit a desk before him with his riding-whip. "The state, L'êtat, c'est moi [I am the state]."
And with these words he turned his back and walked out. And the
assembly could do nothing else but obey and make taxes higher.

「國家，國家，」Louis 怒吼著，用馬鞭猛擊面前的桌子。「國家，L'etat, c'est moi（朕即國家）。」說完這句話，他轉身走了出去。議會除了服從和加稅之外別無選擇。

These words, "L'êtat, c'est moi" have become famous in history, for
they give a picture of how a King of France thought of himself. He
thought of himself as a kind of sun which spreads light everywhere. If
somebody speaks of light, the sun could say: "What are you talking
about: I am the light, I am the source of light." And, in the same way,
Louis XIV thought of himself: "I am France, I am the state of France, and
my glory is the glory of France." And naturally expected his courtiers to
call him le Roi Soleil, the "Sun-King."

這句「L'etat, c'est moi」在歷史上變得赫赫有名，因為它描繪了一位法國國王如何看待自己。他把自己想像成一輪太陽，向四方散發光芒。如果有人談論光，太陽可以說：「你在說什麼？我就是光，我就是光的源頭。」同樣地，Louis 十四也這樣看待自己：「我就是法國，我就是法蘭西國家，我的榮耀就是法國的榮耀。」因此他理所當然地期望朝臣們稱他為 le Roi Soleil——「太陽王」。

As far as Louis XIV was concerned, everything that was done in the
whole of France had only one purpose: to glorify himself, the King of
France. The peasants who worked in the fields, the tradesmen who
worked in the cities, the merchants who did business, they all had one
duty and one purpose alone: to provide the monarch with the money he
needed and with the luxuries such a king must have.

在 Louis 十四看來，整個法國所做的一切只有一個目的：榮耀他自己——法國國王。在田裡勞作的農民、在城裡工作的匠人、做生意的商人，他們統統只有一個職責和一個目的：為國王提供他所需的金錢和這樣一位國王必須擁有的奢華。

The painters, the sculptors, the architects had only one duty, one purpose: to surround His Majesty, Louis XIV, with beauty and to glorify the
greatness of the King in pictures, statues and buildings.

畫家、雕塑家、建築師也只有一個職責、一個目的：用美來環繞 Louis 十四陛下，用繪畫、雕像和建築來頌揚國王的偉大。

The noblemen, knights and lords of France had only one duty, one
purpose in life: to surround the King, amuse and entertain him, serve
him on hand and foot and enjoy the great privilege only granted to men
and women of noble birth: to be in the presence on the Roi Soleil, the
Sun-King. Similarly the soldiers of France only had one duty and one purpose: to fight for the Roi Soleil, to make new conquests for him and, by
these conquests, bring greater glory to the King of France.

法國的貴族、騎士和領主在生命中也只有一個職責、一個目的：圍繞在國王身邊，逗他開心、取悅他，對他百般侍候，並享有只授予貴族出身之男女的崇高特權：能夠待在太陽王 Roi Soleil 身旁。同樣地，法國的士兵也只有一個職責和一個目的：為太陽王而戰，為他開拓新的疆土，並藉由這些征服為法國國王帶來更大的榮耀。

For the
sake of this glory through conquests, Louis XIV plunged
France into one war after another. In 1667 his armies invaded Holland -
for no reason other than the King's glory. But the Dutch called a powerful ally to their aid: the sea. They opened dikes and though the floods
ruined the land for years, they saved Holland from being conquered by
France.

為了追求征服帶來的榮耀，Louis 十四把法國拖入一場又一場戰爭。1667 年他的軍隊入侵 Holland——除了國王的榮耀之外毫無理由。但荷蘭人召來了一個強大的盟友：大海。他們打開了堤防，洪水雖然毀壞了國土多年，卻使 Holland 免於被法國征服。

Another war which Louis XIV started was the War of Succession. It was
a war for the crown of Spain. The King of Spain had died without children
- and Louis XIV claimed the throne of Spain for himself.

Louis 十四發動的另一場戰爭是王位繼承戰爭。這是一場爭奪西班牙王冠的戰爭。西班牙國王死後無嗣——Louis 十四便為自己聲索了西班牙王位。

Now if Spain and France had become one country it would not only
have been the most powerful in Europe but it would also have ruled the
great Spanish colonies in South America, and the great colony of Canada
which was at that time belonged to France. It would have been an
empire of enormous power - and Britain and Germany were afraid that,
sooner or later, Louis with his mad ambition, would conquer them too.
So Britain and Germany joined forces and fought against Louis to prevent him from becoming ruler of France and Spain.

如果西班牙和法國合併為一個國家，它不僅會成為歐洲最強大的國家，還會統治南美洲龐大的西班牙殖民地，以及當時屬於法國的加拿大殖民地。那將是一個擁有巨大權力的帝國——英國和德國擔心 Louis 遲早會憑他瘋狂的野心把他們也征服。於是英國和德國聯手對抗 Louis，阻止他成為法國和西班牙的共同統治者。

This was the "War of Succession" that lasted for ten years. This war
raged not only in Europe, but also in America. In America too the English
colonists and the French colonists fought against each other.

這就是持續了十年的「王位繼承戰爭」。這場戰爭不僅在歐洲肆虐，也蔓延到了美洲。在美洲，英國殖民者和法國殖民者同樣互相廝殺。

If this was not enough, Louis XIV, who called himself the "most Christian King" called the Muslim Turks to his aid. In 1683 the Turks came over
the Balkan peninsula and invaded Austria and laid siege to Vienna. But
Vienna held out, and the Turks were driven back.

好像這還不夠似的，自稱「最虔誠基督教國王」的 Louis 十四竟然召來穆斯林土耳其人作為援軍。1683 年，土耳其人越過 Balkan 半島入侵 Austria，圍攻 Vienna。但 Vienna 堅守住了，土耳其人被擊退。

In the end France lost. The British took a large part of Canada (although not yet all of it) from France. And although Louis XIV was permitted to make his grandson King of Spain it was on condition that
France and Spain would never become one country under one ruler.

最終法國戰敗了。英國從法國手中奪取了加拿大的大部分（雖然還不是全部）。儘管 Louis 十四被允許讓他的孫子成為西班牙國王，但條件是法國和西班牙永遠不能在同一位統治者下合併為一國。

But this war had not only cost thousands of human lives, it had also
cost money. For the sake of his glory Louis XIV had almost ruined France.
Yet he continued to live in greater luxury than any other monarch, he
was still le Roi Soleil, the Sun-King, who said, "L'êtat, c'est moi."

然而這場戰爭不僅奪去了數千條人命，還耗費了大量金錢。為了他的榮耀，Louis 十四幾乎把法國掏空了。但他仍然過著比任何其他君主都更加奢華的生活，他仍然是那位太陽王 le Roi Soleil，那個說「朕即國家」的人。

Chapter 19
Versailles

第十九章
Versailles 凡爾賽宮

Louis XIV simply commanded his parliament, the Estates General, to increase the taxes because he wanted a new palace, and squashed any
objections by his words: "I am the state."

Louis 十四僅僅是命令他的議會——三級會議——加稅，因為他想要一座新宮殿，並用一句「朕即國家」壓下了所有反對意見。

There was already a large royal palace in Paris, but this was not good
enough for the Sun-King. He wanted another palace outside Paris, a
palace surrounded by great parks, and, as it was his wish, the new Palace
of Versailles was built just outside Paris in 1682.

Paris 已經有一座大型皇家宮殿，但對太陽王來說還不夠好。他想要在 Paris 城外再建一座宮殿，一座被大片園林環繞的宮殿。既然這是他的心願，新的 Versailles 宮就於 1682 年在 Paris 郊外建成了。

It is a magnificent palace and even today no visitor to Paris will miss
the opportunity to go and see it. The palace is so large that when the
King was in residence there, ten thousand people - noblemen and their
servants - lived in it. All the noblemen had their own private rooms, and
there are, of course, enormous halls where they came together and
where the King gave audience. The walls and ceilings are covered with
pictures painted by the best artists of France. Big chandeliers (candleholders for hundreds of candles) hang from the ceilings, and these
chandeliers are made of solid silver. There are also chairs made of solid
silver and every piece of furniture - tables, cupboards, beds - is a work of
art, carved by craftsmen and inlaid with gold and silver, and there is the
famous Hall of Mirrors that looks endless.

這是一座宏偉的宮殿，即使在今天，任何造訪 Paris 的遊客都不會錯過去參觀它的機會。宮殿大到當國王住在那裡時，一萬人——貴族和他們的僕人——同時住在裡面。所有貴族都有自己的私人房間，當然還有巨大的廳堂讓他們聚會以及國王接見群臣。牆壁和天花板覆滿了法國最優秀藝術家繪製的畫作。巨大的枝形吊燈（可容納數百根蠟燭的燭台）懸掛在天花板上，這些吊燈全是純銀打造的。還有純銀製成的椅子，每一件家具——桌子、櫃子、床——都是藝術品，由工匠雕刻並鑲嵌金銀。還有那著名的鏡廳，看起來彷彿沒有盡頭。

The vast grounds which surround the Palace of Versailles are just as
impressive. The lawns are all laid out in perfect squares and rectangles
and so all roads and paths run absolutely straight. Louis XIV and his gardeners had a passion for improving on nature and so even the trees at
Versailles are not allowed to grow naturally but are, to this day, cut to
regular shapes - cones, pyramids - so that they look like examples from
geometry books.

環繞 Versailles 宮的廣闊庭園同樣令人讚歎。草坪全部被規劃成完美的正方形和長方形，所有道路和小徑都筆直延伸。Louis 十四和他的園丁們熱衷於「改進」自然，因此 Versailles 的樹木甚至不被允許自然生長，至今仍被修剪成規則的形狀——圓錐形、金字塔形——看起來就像幾何課本裡的範例。

All over these enormous gardens of Versailles there are statues and
also many fountains which used to play continuously at the time of Louis
XIV. Nowadays the fountains only flow on special national holidays - because it is too expensive to keep them going all the time - but for Louis
XIV expense did not matter and the fountains sprayed water day and
night, all the year.

在 Versailles 這些巨大的花園裡到處都是雕像，還有許多噴泉，在 Louis 十四的時代它們曾不停地噴水。如今噴泉只在特別的國定假日才開放——因為讓它們一直運作太昂貴了——但對 Louis 十四來說，花費並不重要，噴泉日夜不停地噴水，整年如此。

Life inside the palace was just as strictly and stiffly regulated as the
lawns and trees: how noblemen of equal rank greeted each other, how
lords and ladies of different rank greeted each other, how each rank had
to greet the King, how one walked, or sat down, and the eating and
drinking and dancing, and how one dressed or played cards - it was all in
accordance with strict rules called etiquette.

宮殿裡的生活就像草坪和樹木一樣嚴格而僵化地被規範著：同等階級的貴族如何互相問候，不同階級的領主和女士如何彼此行禮，各個階級如何向國王致敬，如何走路、如何坐下，吃喝跳舞的方式，穿著打扮和玩牌的規矩——一切都遵循著被稱為「禮儀」的嚴格規則。

It was part of these strict rules that the highest noblemen of France,
lords and dukes acted as personal servants to the King; - no ordinary
servants were allowed to attend to the King but only men of noble
blood.

這些嚴格規則的一部分是：法國最高等級的貴族——領主和公爵——充當國王的貼身僕人；不允許普通僕人侍候國王，只有貴族血統的人才有資格。

Let us begin in the morning, with the "getting up," in French le lever. It
sounds incredible, but this getting up was a great ceremony. At eight
o'clock, the time when the King woke up, a duke presented him with his
slippers and a prince handed him his dressing-gown. Then two noblemen brought him his prayer book and the King was left alone a few minutes to say his morning prayers.

讓我們從早晨說起，從「起床」開始，法語叫 le lever。聽起來難以置信，但這個起床竟然是一場盛大的儀式。八點鐘，也就是國王醒來的時間，一位公爵呈上他的拖鞋，一位王子遞上他的晨袍。然後兩位貴族把祈禱書送來，國王獨自待幾分鐘做晨禱。

Then a number of noblemen were allowed to be present while the
King was shaved by a barber (who was not a nobleman). When the shaving was over, they all left and a new batch of noblemen came to help the
King to dress.

接著一批貴族被允許在場，國王由一位理髮師（不是貴族）刮鬍子。刮完鬍子後，他們全部退出，新一批貴族進來幫助國王穿衣。

Only the King's own brother had the privilege of handing him his
shirt, then a lesser lord gave him his breeches (knee-length trousers),
and another
prince handed him his wig. Two noblemen (each holding
one sleeve) helped the King into his coat, two others put his shoes on his
feet, and there were still other noblemen to bring him his hat and
gloves, and walking stick and a handkerchief.

只有國王的親兄弟才有特權遞上他的襯衫，然後一位較低階的貴族遞上他的馬褲（及膝長褲），另一位王子遞上他的假髮。兩位貴族（各持一隻袖子）幫國王穿上外套，另外兩位為他穿上鞋子，還有其他貴族負責送上他的帽子和手套、手杖和手帕。

In all this ceremony there is no mentioning of a wash - because neither the King nor his noblemen either washed or took a bath, ever. They
used perfumes, scent, to hide the smell of their unwashed bodies.

在這整套儀式中完全沒有提到洗漱——因為無論是國王還是他的貴族們從來不洗澡，也不沐浴。他們用香水、香料來掩蓋身上不洗澡的體味。

Then at meal times it was again lords and noblemen who each served
a different course to the King and the royal family; and there was one
nobleman who had the special duty of handing the King a napkin as
often as it was needed - which was quite often as the King, like everyone
in those days, used only a knife and his fingers.

到了用餐時間，又是領主和貴族們各自為國王和王室家族端上不同的菜餚；還有一位貴族的特殊職責是隨時遞給國王餐巾——而這需要相當頻繁，因為國王和那個時代的所有人一樣，只用一把刀和手指進食。

The meals of His Majesty, Louis XIV, were enormous; for his midday
meal he used to eat four large plates of soup, a whole pheasant or duck,
two large helpings of ham, then came a plate of mutton, then four or five
hard boiled eggs, and finally came cakes and fruit.

Louis 十四陛下的飯量極為驚人；他的午餐通常是四大盤湯、一整隻雉雞或鴨子、兩大份火腿，然後是一盤羊肉，接著是四五顆水煮蛋，最後是蛋糕和水果。

The morning - that is the time between le lever and lunch - was the
time when the King attended to the business of government: he saw his
ministers, dictated letters and received ambassadors of foreign countries.
But no such serious matters were allowed to disturb the afternoon or
evening. The afternoon was given to the King's favourite sport: hunting.
And for the evening there was again a complete change of dress - with
the same ceremony as in the morning. All the courtiers too had to
change and appear in evening clothes of silk or cloth of gold. The
evening's entertainment was usually dancing and gambling and lasted till
one o'clock at night. Then the King went to bed and his undressing was
again attended and helped by chosen noblemen.

早上——也就是 le lever 到午餐之間——是國王處理政務的時間：他接見大臣、口述信件、接待外國使節。但下午和晚上不允許這些嚴肅事務來打擾。下午留給國王最喜歡的運動：打獵。到了晚上又要完全換一套衣服——和早上一樣的儀式。所有朝臣也必須換裝，穿上絲綢或金線織成的晚禮服出場。晚間的娛樂通常是跳舞和賭博，一直持續到凌晨一點。然後國王就寢，他的脫衣過程又由被挑選出來的貴族侍候和幫忙。

The cut and style of the dresses worn by lords and ladies changed
continuously according to the whims and fancies of the King. But whatever the courtiers wore became immediately the fashion - first in Versailles, then in Paris, then in France and soon in the rest of the world. If
Louis XIV decided one day to wear lace at his shirt-cuffs, a few months
later every man in Europe and in America (who could afford it) wore lace
on his cuffs.

領主和女士們穿的衣服款式不斷隨著國王的心血來潮而改變。但朝臣們穿什麼，立刻就成了時尚——先在 Versailles，然後在 Paris，然後在全法國，很快就傳遍了全世界。如果 Louis 十四某天決定在襯衫袖口戴蕾絲，幾個月後歐洲和美洲每一個（負擔得起的）男人都在袖口上戴蕾絲了。

The thousands of courtiers - the noble lords and ladies who surrounded the King and served him on hand and foot - competed with
one another in flattering him, and went to any length to amuse and to
please him.

數以千計的朝臣——那些環繞在國王身旁百般侍候的貴族男女——競相奉承他，想盡一切辦法逗他開心、取悅他。

At one time Louis XIV stayed as a guest with one of his noblemen, the
Duke d'Autin. The King looked out of the splendid room given to him and
mentioned to his host that the trees of a forest in the distance rather
spoiled the fine view one had from that room. The next morning as the
King looked out - the whole forest had disappeared. The peasants of the
Duke had worked all night to cut down and remove the trees which had
displeased His Majesty.

有一次 Louis 十四作為客人住在他的一位貴族 d'Autin 公爵家中。國王從分給他的華麗房間往外望，對主人提到遠處一片森林的樹木頗為破壞了從這個房間望出去的美景。第二天早上國王再往外看時——整片森林消失了。公爵的農民們連夜工作，砍倒並移走了讓陛下不悅的樹木。

You can imagine that keeping not only the King and the royal family
but a whole royal court of thousands of noblemen in such luxury cost a
lot of money. And where did all this money come from? From the peasants of France. They did not live in luxury, they lived in dirty hovels, they
walked about in rags, they were hungry and often near starvation, but it
was their hard work which had to provide the King and his court with the
luxuries, the fine dresses, the enormous meals, the splendid palace.

你可以想像，維持不僅是國王和王室，還有數千名貴族組成的整個宮廷過著如此奢華的生活，需要花多少錢。而這些錢都從哪裡來？來自法國的農民。他們並不生活在奢華中，他們住在骯髒的棚屋裡，穿著破爛的衣衫，他們飢餓且時常瀕臨餓死的邊緣，但正是他們的辛勤勞動必須為國王和宮廷提供奢侈品、華服、豐盛的筵席和富麗堂皇的宮殿。

The Royal Tax Collectors were officials whose duty it was to raise the
money from the people. They came, accompanied by soldiers - and if a
peasant did not have the money for the tax ready, the soldiers took away
his last cow or sheep or even his plough. The fact that this peasant would
not have any money the next year either - as he could not till his land -
did not matter. Not did it matter if he and his family died of hunger: the
peasant, like the courtiers, existed only for the King's pleasure.

皇家稅吏是負責向人民徵收錢財的官員。他們在士兵的陪同下前來——如果一個農民沒有準備好稅款，士兵就會拿走他最後一頭牛、一隻羊，甚至他的犁。至於這個農民第二年也不會有錢——因為他沒辦法耕地了——那無關緊要。他和他的家人是否餓死也無關緊要：農民就像朝臣一樣，只為國王的享樂而存在。

So, with the reign of Louis XIV, there begins a time in France when a
few thousand people - the King and his court - lived in fantastic luxury,
while millions of ordinary people became poorer and poorer.

因此，隨著 Louis 十四的統治，法國開始了一個時代：幾千人——國王和他的宮廷——過著極盡奢華的生活，而數百萬普通人卻越來越窮。

But it was not only the luxuries of Versailles the peasants had to pay
for, but also the vainglorious wars Louis XIV started. It was the peasants'
taxes which paid for the weapons and guns and for the soldiers' pay, and
it was the peasants' sons who marched and fought and died in battle for
the glory of Louis XIV.

但農民們必須支付的不僅是 Versailles 的奢侈生活，還有 Louis 十四發動的虛榮戰爭。是農民的稅金支付了武器、槍砲和士兵的軍餉，是農民的兒子們行軍、戰鬥並在戰場上為 Louis 十四的榮耀而死。

Chapter 20
Banking

第二十章
銀行業

The extravagance, the sheer waste of money at the court of Louis XIV, in
contrast to the plight of the peasant population, might lead one to think
that this is a period in the history of France which people recall only with
shame. But it is not so, because it is also a time in which some very great
Frenchmen lived - great painters, architects, poets and playwrights. Just
as the time of Queen Elizabeth was a time of great Englishmen like
Drake, Raleigh, Shakespeare, one hundred years later in the time of
Louis XIV, there were great Frenchmen.

Louis 十四宮廷的奢靡與金錢的徹底浪費，與農民的悲慘處境形成鮮明對比，可能會讓人認為這是法國歷史上人們只帶著羞恥回憶的時期。但事實並非如此，因為這也是一個出現了偉大法國人的時代——偉大的畫家、建築師、詩人和劇作家。就像 Elizabeth 女王時代是 Drake、Raleigh、Shakespeare 等偉大英國人輩出的時代一樣，一百年後的 Louis 十四時代也有偉大的法國人。

One of the great men of that time was Molière, who wrote comedies,
funny plays which are still performed today in France, as we perform
Shakespeare's plays. There is one play (Le Malade Imaginaire) in which
Molière makes fun of a man who is so fussy about his health that he
imagines he suffers from every possible and impossible illness. In another play (Le Bourgeois Gentilhomme) he makes fun of an ordinary man
who tries to imitate the manners of noble courtiers. They are plays one
can still enjoy today.

那個時代的偉人之一是 Moliere，他寫喜劇——有趣的戲劇，至今仍在法國演出，就像我們演出 Shakespeare 的戲劇一樣。有一齣戲（Le Malade Imaginaire《乞丐貴族》……不，應說是《無病呻吟》）裡，Moliere 嘲笑一個對自己的健康過度操心的人，他幻想自己患上了所有可能和不可能的疾病。在另一齣戲（Le Bourgeois Gentilhomme《貴人迷》）中，他嘲笑一個試圖模仿宮廷貴族舉止的普通人。這些戲劇至今仍令人樂在其中。

It was no merit of Louis XIV that gave rise to such great painters and
writers and architects existing in his time, but he did support them and
paid them and gave them work to do, because of course, it helped to
glorify himself.

這些偉大的畫家、作家和建築師出現在他的時代並非 Louis 十四的功勞，但他確實資助了他們、付他們報酬、給他們工作，因為這當然有助於榮耀他自己。

But for the poor hungry peasants of that time, the clever plays, the
beautiful paintings, the splendid palace of Versailles meant very little.
One day a howling mob of hungry people in rags surrounded the Palace
of Versailles, shouting, "Bread, Bread, give us bread!" But the soldiers of
the Palace Guard came and drove them away. The people did not forget
how little the King cared for their poverty and suffering when Louis XIV
died in 1715, the mob of Paris shouted curses at the funeral procession.
The common people of France were glad at the news that le Roi Soleil
had died.

但對那個時代飢餓的窮苦農民來說，精巧的戲劇、美麗的畫作、輝煌的 Versailles 宮殿幾乎毫無意義。有一天，一群衣衫襤褸、飢餓嚎叫的暴民包圍了 Versailles 宮，高喊：「麵包，麵包，給我們麵包！」但宮殿衛兵的士兵趕來把他們驅散了。人民沒有忘記國王多麼不在乎他們的貧困和苦難——當 Louis 十四在 1715 年去世時，Paris 的民眾對著送葬隊伍破口大罵。法國的平民百姓聽到太陽王已死的消息時感到慶幸。

Louis XIV had outlived his son and grandson, they both died before
him - and his great-grandson was only five years old at the death of le
Roi Soleil. The little boy's uncle, Philip, Duke of Orleans, was appointed as
"Regent," as it is called, to rule temporarily, in the name of the King, but
not as King. However, Philip, the Regent, had been a courtier of le Roi
Soleil for too long to care for anything but festivities and
amusements,
and his only worry now was where to find money for his own pleasures
and those of the court.

Louis 十四比他的兒子和孫子都活得長，他們都死在他前面——太陽王去世時，他的曾孫才五歲。這個小男孩的叔叔 Philip，Orleans 公爵，被任命為所謂的「攝政王」，代表國王暫時統治，但不是以國王的身分。然而 Philip 這位攝政王在太陽王身邊當朝臣當了太久，除了慶典和娛樂之外什麼都不在意，他此刻唯一的擔心就是去哪裡找錢來滿足自己和宮廷的享樂。

It was at this time that John Law, a Scotsman from Edinburgh,
brought a new idea to France.

就在這個時候，來自 Edinburgh 的蘇格蘭人 John Law 把一個新想法帶到了法國。

France had colonies; she still had a large part of Canada as well as a
part of North America called Louisiana (in honour of Louis XIV). These
colonies could be developed - so said John Law - and they could bring
money to France. But first money had to be put in, men had to be sent
out, with ships, arms, equipment. In time though, every pound spent on
the colonies would come back twofold and threefold.

法國有殖民地；她仍然擁有加拿大的大部分以及北美洲一個叫 Louisiana 的地方（以 Louis 十四命名）。這些殖民地可以開發——John Law 這樣說——它們能為法國帶來財富。但首先得投入資金，派人出去，帶上船隻、武器和裝備。假以時日，每投入殖民地的一英鎊都會加倍、甚至三倍地回來。

And all one had to do was - not tax the people - but borrow the
money from them, with the promise to pay it back later when the
colonies flourished, and every man who lent the Government one pound
could be certain to receive two or three pounds later, in return.

而需要做的就是——不是向人民徵稅——而是向他們借錢，承諾等殖民地繁榮後再還，每個借給政府一英鎊的人都能確信日後會收到兩三英鎊的回報。

It seemed a wonderful idea, the Regent Philip agreed and John Law
opened a bank in Paris in 1716. This bank offered the people papers
which were called bank notes. If you wanted such a bank note for one
pound you had to pay the bank a gold coin worth one pound. But - incredibly - this same banknote would later on, be worth two pounds, or
three pounds - or even ten pounds which would be repaid in gold again,
within a few years. Nowadays people buy "shares" in a company - it is the
same thing. You buy a share for £1 - and you hope and expect that the
company will make profits and these profits (a share of them) will be paid
to you and make the £1 worth £1.10, £1.50 or even £5. Sometimes it happens, and sometimes it doesn't. It is a gamble.

這看似一個絕妙的主意，攝政王 Philip 同意了，John Law 於 1716 年在 Paris 開了一家銀行。這家銀行向人們提供被稱為「銀行券」的紙張。如果你想要一張面值一英鎊的銀行券，你得付給銀行一枚價值一英鎊的金幣。但——令人難以置信的是——同一張銀行券日後會值兩英鎊、三英鎊——甚至十英鎊，並在幾年內以黃金償還。如今人們購買公司的「股份」——其實是一回事。你花一英鎊買一份股票——你期望公司會獲利，這些利潤（你的那一份）會付給你，讓一英鎊變成一鎊一角、一鎊五角甚至五英鎊。有時候會如此，有時候不會。這是一場賭博。

In those days, however, it was something quite new, and people fell
for it. John Law himself believed honestly that the gamble would come
off - that the French colonies would become very profitable. And the
French people believed it too. The peasants had no money, but in the
cities and towns of France, people had money, people had savings - and
they came in their thousands and bought John Law's banknotes. In fact,
there was soon a trade in these banknotes. A man who bought a banknote for £1 could sell it to another man a month later for £2, because
that other man now expected to get much more for it. And all those who
gave up their little savings for these bits of paper saw themselves as
millionaires in a year or two.

然而在那個年代，這是全新的事物，人們紛紛上當了。John Law 本人真心相信這場賭博會成功——法國的殖民地會變得非常有利可圖。法國人也相信了。農民沒有錢，但在法國的城鎮裡，人們有錢、有積蓄——他們成千上萬地湧來購買 John Law 的銀行券。事實上，很快就出現了銀行券的交易。一個人花一英鎊買的銀行券，一個月後可以用兩英鎊賣給另一個人，因為那個人預期它會更值錢。所有那些把微薄積蓄換成這些紙片的人都把自己想像成一兩年後的百萬富翁。

But, of course, it was all a dream. The French colonies did not produce
such great profits, and certainly not as fast as people expected. A few
clever people did not wait very long - they soon came to John Law and
said: "Here are our £1 notes, we want as promised £2 or £3 in gold," and
they got it. But, of course, there was only enough real money in the Bank
to satisfy the first few who came, and no money at all for those who
came later, they could not even get their £1 back. There was a panic:
everybody wanted to sell banknotes, and no one wanted to buy them,
and the notes became worthless.

但這當然全是一場夢。法國的殖民地並沒有產生那麼大的利潤，當然也沒有人們預期的那麼快。幾個聰明人沒有等太久——他們很快去找 John Law 說：「這是我們的一英鎊銀行券，我們要按照承諾拿回兩英鎊或三英鎊的黃金」，他們拿到了。但當然，銀行裡只有足夠的真金白銀來滿足最先來的那幾個人，後來的人什麼都拿不到，他們連自己的一英鎊都拿不回來。恐慌爆發了：每個人都想賣掉銀行券，沒人想買，銀行券變得一文不值。

Hundreds of thousands of people had lost all their savings and John
Law had to flee from France. He had not made any money for himself,
but the idea had not worked and the people blamed him for it. He died
in poverty in Italy in 1729.

數十萬人失去了全部積蓄，John Law 不得不逃離法國。他自己並沒有從中賺到錢，但這個構想失敗了，人們把責任歸咎於他。他於 1729 年在意大利貧困潦倒地死去。

But the idea of bank notes - of giving people paper instead of gold
coins, this idea was later taken up by all governments, and in our time
gold coins have disappeared altogether.

但銀行券的概念——給人們紙張代替金幣——這個想法後來被所有政府所採用，在我們的時代，金幣已經完全消失了。

The people of France had no love for the Regent Philip who had let
John Law start this disastrous experiment. And they rejoiced in 1722
when the true successor, the great-grandson of the Roi Soleil became
King Louis XV at the age of thirteen.

法國人民對讓 John Law 啟動這場災難性實驗的攝政王 Philip 毫無好感。而他們在 1722 年歡欣鼓舞，因為真正的繼承人——太陽王的曾孫——以十三歲之齡成為 Louis 十五國王。

He was a handsome young boy, and no king had a better welcome;
the people called him le bien aimée, "the well-beloved." They expected of
him he would put things right and do something about the widespread
poverty.

他是一個英俊的少年，沒有哪位國王受到過更好的歡迎；人民稱他為 le bien aime——「深受愛戴者」。他們期望他能撥亂反正，對普遍的貧困採取行動。

But Louis XV had a most unfortunate upbringing: he had been spoiled
and petted, he had been flattered and praised and fussed over, and as a
result he knew only one rule of life: his own pleasure, his own satisfaction.
His private tutors - instead of teaching him - played cards with him,
because he liked it better than work. And as soon as he was old enough
to hunt he gave up his lessons altogether. What need had a king to learn
anything? Once Louis XV was crowned King he found the job of governing France just as boring as lessons - and left that tedious job to others.

但 Louis 十五的教養極為不幸：他被溺愛和嬌寵，被奉承、讚美和呵護，結果他只懂得一條人生準則：自己的快樂、自己的滿足。他的私人教師——非但不教他——反而陪他打牌，因為他覺得打牌比學習有趣。一到可以打獵的年紀，他就徹底放棄了功課。一個國王有什麼必要學任何東西呢？Louis 十五加冕為王之後，發現治理法國和上課一樣無聊——便把這個煩人的工作留給了別人。

At the age of seventeen he was married to a Polish princess - but he
got soon tired of his wife and found one of the court ladies more attractive. The name of this lady was Madame de Pompadour. She was beautiful and clever, and ambitious. She wanted to rule France, and lazy,
spineless Louis XV was only too pleased to let her.

十七歲時他娶了一位波蘭公主——但他很快就對妻子厭倦了，發現宮廷中一位女士更有魅力。這位女士的名字叫 Madame de Pompadour。她美麗、聰明而且野心勃勃。她想要統治法國，而懶散無骨氣的 Louis 十五求之不得地讓她去做。

And so it came that France was ruled by the King's mistress, Madame
de Pompadour. Louis XV said yes to all she wanted, put his signature to
any document she put before him, and took no interest in what happened to France or in France. He gave his time entirely to amusements,
festivities, games, and suffered only from one thing: boredom.

就這樣，法國被國王的情婦 Madame de Pompadour 所統治。Louis 十五對她想要的一切都說好，在她放到面前的任何文件上簽名，對法國發生的事或法國的命運毫無興趣。他把全部時間都用在娛樂、慶典、遊戲上，唯一讓他感到痛苦的是：無聊。

Chapter 21
Louis XV and Frederick the Great

第二十一章
Louis 十五與 Frederick 大帝

Louis XV had been completely spoiled from early childhood. No effort
had ever been demanded of him, no wish had ever been denied to him,
so he grew up into a lazy good-for-nothing who was, however, King of
France - a king with absolute power. And as Louis XV was too lazy to rule
and to shoulder the burden and worries of government, he left his absolute power in the hands of his lady-love, Madame de Pompadour. You
can see how the upbringing of a king, especially this utterly ineffectual
upbringing could influence the fate of a whole nation.

Louis 十五從小就被徹底寵壞了。從來沒有人要求他付出任何努力，從來沒有人拒絕過他的任何願望，於是他長成了一個懶惰的廢物——但偏偏是法國國王，一個擁有絕對權力的國王。由於 Louis 十五太懶而不願統治，不願承擔治國的重擔和憂慮，他把絕對權力交到了他的情人 Madame de Pompadour 手中。你可以看到一位國王的教養——尤其是這種完全失敗的教養——如何能夠影響一整個國家的命運。

At the same time as Louis XV there was another king whose upbringing was the very opposite: it was a harsh and strict upbringing. But this
king is given a designation few kings are ever given - he is called "the
Great" and so belongs to the illustrious company of Alexander, Charlemagne (French for Charles the Great) and Alfred.

與 Louis 十五同時代的還有另一位國王，他的教養恰恰相反：那是一種嚴苛而嚴格的教養。但這位國王獲得了很少有國王能獲得的稱號——他被稱為「大帝」，因此躋身於 Alexander、Charlemagne（法語的 Charles 大帝）和 Alfred 這些顯赫的行列。

This king was Frederick the Great of Prussia. Prussia was a little kingdom in the North of Germany and, in theory, the kings of Prussia owed
obedience to the Emperor of Germany who had his capital in Vienna. But
this was only in theory. In reality, the little kingdoms of Germany - Prussia, Bavaria,
Saxony - were quite independent and even made war
against the Emperor, if and when, they wanted to.

這位國王就是 Prussia 的 Frederick 大帝。Prussia 是德國北部一個小王國，理論上 Prussia 國王必須效忠定都於 Vienna 的德意志皇帝。但這僅僅是理論上的。實際上，德國的小王國們——Prussia、Bavaria、Saxony——相當獨立，只要他們想，甚至會對皇帝開戰。

Frederick the Great's father, Frederick William was a rough-mannered
man who despised elegant clothes and fine manners - the lifestyle of the
French court. Frederick William had quite a different ambition: to have
the best fighting men, the best soldiers in the world.

Frederick 大帝的父親 Frederick William 是一個粗獷的人，鄙視華麗的服飾和優雅的舉止——也就是法國宮廷的生活方式。Frederick William 有著完全不同的抱負：擁有世界上最能打仗的人、最好的士兵。

The idea of army drill was taken to the limits by Frederick William: Soldiers should stand "to attention" as stiff as ram-rods; whole regiments
should march, present arms, or turn about like one man and with the
precision of machines. His soldiers were truly trained in this manner, and
once a soldier was used to obeying commands as though he were a machine, he would also obey in battle, without thinking, even if it cost him
his life. The soldiers of Prussia were drilled for absolute obedience.

Frederick William 把軍事操練推到了極致：士兵們立正時要像鐵桿一樣筆直；整個團必須像一個人一樣行進、舉槍致敬或轉向，精確得像機器。他的士兵們確實是這樣訓練的，一旦士兵習慣了像機器一樣服從命令，他在戰場上也會不假思索地服從，即使賠上性命也在所不惜。Prussia 的士兵被操練出了絕對的服從。

But King Frederick William also demanded the same obedience, the
same discipline, from his son, Frederick.

但 Frederick William 國王也要求他的兒子 Frederick 表現出同樣的服從和紀律。

Frederick did not enjoy this at all; young Frederick did not like the
rigid discipline and the rough manners of his father. He preferred the
fine manners, the elegant fashions of France. And when his father made
him an officer in the Prussian army at the age of sixteen, Frederick made
plans with another officer to run away, to flee from Prussia and to go to
France.

Frederick 完全不喜歡這一切；年輕的 Frederick 不喜歡父親的嚴格紀律和粗魯舉止。他更喜歡法國的優雅禮儀和時尚。當他父親在他十六歲時讓他成為 Prussia 軍隊的軍官後，Frederick 和另一位軍官策劃逃跑，逃離 Prussia，前往法國。

But the plan was uncovered, and the old King would not tolerate such
disobedience. The officer who had been willing to help Prince Frederick
was condemned to death, and Frederick had to watch a squad of soldiers
put his friend against a wall and shoot him.

但計劃被發現了，老國王不能容忍這種違抗。那位願意幫助 Frederick 王子的軍官被判處死刑，Frederick 不得不眼看著一隊士兵把他的朋友推到牆前槍決。

It was a terrible experience for young Frederick - but it taught him
that there was no escape from his duties and from obedience to his father's wishes. He became an efficient officer and, later, an outstanding
general. But this experience of seeing his best friend shot also made him
a hard man, a man who had little feeling for others.

這對年輕的 Frederick 來說是一次可怕的經歷——但它教會了他：無法逃避自己的責任，也無法逃避對父親意志的服從。他成了一名稱職的軍官，後來成為一位傑出的將軍。但目睹最好的朋友被槍殺的經歷也使他變成了一個冷酷的人，一個對別人缺乏感情的人。

Many years later, in a battle, when Frederick's soldiers were mown
down by heavy cannon-fire, their iron discipline broke and they turned
to flee, Frederick shouted furiously at them "You dogs, do you want to
live for ever?" The men turned round and continued the attack.

多年後，在一場戰役中，當 Frederick 的士兵被猛烈的砲火掃倒，他們的鐵紀律崩潰，轉身想逃跑時，Frederick 怒吼道：「你們這些狗，難道想永遠活著嗎？」士兵們轉回身，繼續進攻。

So, when the old King, Frederick William, died in 1740 and Frederick
became King of Prussia, this young King was a very different person from
the soft, lazy and spoiled Louis XV who had never known any hardship in
his life.

因此，當老國王 Frederick William 在 1740 年去世、Frederick 成為 Prussia 國王時，這位年輕的國王和從未經歷過任何艱難的軟弱、懶惰、被慣壞的 Louis 十五截然不同。

Of course, both kinds of upbringing are wrong - the merciless discipline imposed on Frederick and the soft petting and spoiling which ruined Louis. It is, really, the same polarity as there was between Puritans
and Cavaliers. Frederick became a man one can respect but not like, and
Louis XV was a person one could like for his charm but not respect.

當然，兩種教養方式都是錯的——對 Frederick 施加的無情紀律和毀掉 Louis 的溺愛嬌寵。這其實和清教徒與騎士黨之間的對立是同一種兩極性。Frederick 成了一個令人尊敬但不討喜的人，而 Louis 十五是一個因其魅力令人喜愛但無法令人尊敬的人。

As soon as Frederick had become King of Prussia he wanted to use
the well-drilled army that was at his command. At the time the Emperor
of Germany had died and was succeeded by his daughter, Maria Theresa,
Empress of Germany and Austria.

Frederick 一成為 Prussia 國王就想動用他手中那支訓練有素的軍隊。當時德國皇帝去世了，繼位的是他的女兒 Maria Theresa，德國和 Austria 的女皇。

Frederick thought that his own Prussia too small and he could make it
bigger by taking a slice away from the lands ruled by the Empress Maria
Theresa. After all, she was "only a woman" and this should make it easier,
so Frederick declared war on the Empress.

Frederick 覺得自己的 Prussia 太小了，可以從 Maria Theresa 女皇統治的土地上割一塊來擴大領土。畢竟她「只是一個女人」，這應該讓事情更容易，於是 Frederick 向女皇宣戰。

But it was not such an easy war as Frederick had expected, for Louis
XV of France - or rather Madame de Pompadour who made the
decisions - came into the war on the side of Maria Theresa.

但這場戰爭並不像 Frederick 預期的那麼容易，因為法國的 Louis 十五——或者說是做決策的 Madame de Pompadour——加入了 Maria Theresa 一方。

Britain, on the other hand, decided to come in on the side of Frederick. And then Russia, too, joined in, on the side of Maria Theresa.
So there was Prussia and Britain on one side, France, Russia, Austria
on the other, and the whole of Europe was thrown into war and bloodshed.

另一方面，英國決定站在 Frederick 這邊。然後俄羅斯也加入了，站在 Maria Theresa 一方。於是 Prussia 和英國在一邊，法國、俄羅斯、Austria 在另一邊，整個歐洲被捲入了戰爭和流血。

And this was not only fought in Europe; on the high seas British and
French ships fought each other; in North America English and French
colonists fought each other and the Indigenous American tribes were
brought in on both sides; it was really a world war.

而且戰爭不僅發生在歐洲；在公海上英國和法國的船艦互相交戰；在北美洲英國和法國的殖民者互相廝殺，原住民部落被拉入了雙方陣營；這真的是一場世界大戰。

It was also a long war - a war that lasted for seven years (1756-63),
thus it is called the Seven Years' War. It cost innumerable lives. It also
cost, like all wars, a great deal of money. And, of all the nations which
took part in this war, France could least afford to spend money on war.
But all this did not worry Louis XV who carried on with his pleasures and
festivities and entertainments in the Palace of Versailles. He left politics
to Madame de Pompadour and the fighting to his generals.

這也是一場漫長的戰爭——持續了七年（1756-63），因此被稱為七年戰爭。它奪去了無數生命。像所有戰爭一樣，它也花費了大量金錢。而在所有參戰國家中，法國最承受不起在戰爭上花錢。但這一切都不讓 Louis 十五擔心，他在 Versailles 宮繼續享樂、慶典和娛樂。他把政治留給 Madame de Pompadour，把打仗留給他的將軍們。

Frederick of Prussia at least commanded his own troops and shared
with them the hardship of war, and it was due to his generalship and
iron determination that, in the end, against the great armies of Austria,
Russia, France, the small but well-trained army of Prussia won. The Seven
Years' War ended with victory for Frederick. It also ended with victory for
Britain which took the remaining parts of Canada from France.

Prussia 的 Frederick 至少親自統率自己的軍隊，與士兵們共同承受戰爭的艱辛。正是由於他的將才和鐵一般的決心，最終 Prussia 那支小而精的軍隊擊敗了 Austria、俄羅斯、法國的龐大軍隊。七年戰爭以 Frederick 的勝利告終。英國也取得了勝利，從法國手中奪取了加拿大的剩餘部分。

But France was now worse off than ever before; she had lost Canada,
she had lost thousands of soldiers killed in battle, and she had wasted
vast amounts of money for nothing.

但法國現在比以往任何時候都更加淒慘；她失去了加拿大，失去了數千名在戰場上陣亡的士兵，還白白浪費了大量金錢。

Yet all this may not have come upon France if Louis XV had been
brought up differently, or if Frederick of Prussia had been brought up
differently.

然而，如果 Louis 十五的教養不同，或者 Prussia 的 Frederick 的教養不同，這一切或許都不會降臨到法國頭上。

Chapter 22
The Count of Saint Germain

第二十二章
Saint Germain 伯爵

You can see from the examples of Louis XV and Frederick the Great of
Prussia that their upbringing influenced the fate of whole nations.
France lost her colonies and became poorer than ever before, but Prussia became the most powerful of the German states.

從 Louis 十五和 Prussia 的 Frederick 大帝的例子中可以看出，他們的教養影響了整個國家的命運。法國失去了殖民地，變得比以往更窮，而 Prussia 則成為德意志諸邦中最強大的。

But it was surely
wrong that the fate of millions, their prosperity or poverty, should depend on the character, the strength or weakness of one man. In ancient
Egypt people had, indeed, accepted good kings or bad kings as the will
of the gods; in ancient Rome people still accepted good Caesars or weak
and evil ones as the will of the gods. In the early Middle Ages the Christian kings were still believed to be the instruments of God's will. But the
new age - the age that began with Leonardo and Columbus and Luther -
this new age could not look up to kings with awe and respect, and, in the
long run, people would not let a single man - just because he was a King
- make decisions about the fate of millions. The time for this absolute
power of kings was past.

但數百萬人的命運——他們的繁榮或貧困——取決於一個人的性格、堅強或軟弱，這無疑是不對的。在古埃及，人們確實把好國王或壞國王都當作神的旨意來接受；在古羅馬，人們仍然把好的或懦弱邪惡的 Caesar 當作神的旨意來接受。在中世紀早期，基督教國王仍被認為是上帝意志的工具。但新的時代——從 Leonardo、Columbus 和 Luther 開始的時代——這個新時代不能再以敬畏和尊崇的目光仰望國王，長遠來看，人們不會讓一個人——僅僅因為他是國王——來決定數百萬人的命運。國王絕對權力的時代已經過去了。

There were people at that time who gave much thought to this
question. The absolute power of kings has to go - but should it be swept
away in chaos and bloodshed, or would it not be better if this great
change came peacefully, step by step?

當時有人對這個問題深思熟慮。國王的絕對權力必須消失——但它應該在混亂和流血中被掃除嗎？如果這個偉大的變革能和平地、一步一步地到來，不是更好嗎？

While most people, if they worry about the future at all, only worry
about their own well-being, or that of their families; in contrast, there
were people who were concerned about the future of Europe and of
humanity
as a whole and they wanted to help so that progress and
change should come without great suffering and bloodshed.

大多數人如果擔心未來，也只是擔心自己或家人的福祉；相比之下，有些人關心的是歐洲和整個人類的未來，他們想要幫助推動進步和變革，使其不需要經歷巨大的苦難和流血。

In the Middle Ages the alchemists searched for the secret of the
Philosophers' Stone, the stone which could change base metals into
gold, which could give a long life, which could cure illness. They were the
"true alchemists" - and only a truly selfless person could ever join the secret brotherhood of the true alchemists. The sign of this secret brotherhood was a black cross from which red roses grew. Now this secret
brotherhood of the Rosicrucians decided to try to avert the terror and
bloodshed they saw coming in Europe, and especially France.

在中世紀，煉金術士們尋找賢者之石的秘密——那塊能把卑金屬變成黃金、能賜予長壽、能治癒疾病的石頭。他們是「真正的煉金術士」——只有真正無私的人才能加入真正煉金術士的秘密兄弟會。這個秘密兄弟會的標誌是一個黑色十字架，上面生長著紅玫瑰。現在，這個 Rosicrucian 秘密兄弟會決定嘗試避免他們預見到的即將降臨歐洲——尤其是法國——的恐怖和流血。

In the year 1760, during the Seven Years' War, a strange man appeared in Paris. He called himself the Count of Saint Germain, but, as he
said himself, it was not his real name and no one has ever found out who
he really was and what his real name was.

1760 年，七年戰爭期間，一個奇異的人出現在 Paris。他自稱 Saint Germain 伯爵，但正如他自己所說，這不是他的真名，至今沒有人查出他究竟是誰、他的真名是什麼。

This strange man, the Count of Saint Germain, seemed to be immensely rich, his exquisite clothes were adorned with great diamonds
and rubies, he gave great parties and banquets for hundreds of people,
and the noble lords and ladies of France flocked to the invitations of this
strange man who seemed to have unlimited money, though no one
could make out where his money came from as he had no business, no
estates, farms, houses.

這個奇異的人，Saint Germain 伯爵，似乎極為富有，他精美的衣服上裝飾著巨大的鑽石和紅寶石。他舉辦盛大的宴會招待數百人，法國的貴族男女紛紛前來赴這位奇人的邀請——他似乎有花不完的錢，但沒有人能搞清楚他的錢從哪裡來，因為他沒有生意、沒有地產、農場或房屋。

What was even stranger was that he looked like a man of about fifty
years of age, but an old lady who came to one of his parties swore she
had seen him when she was a young girl, some forty years earlier in Italy,
and he had then looked just the same - a man of about fifty years of age.
So imagine how puzzled people were by this mysterious stranger who
called himself the Count of Saint Germain.

更奇怪的是，他看上去像一個五十歲左右的人，但一位來參加他宴會的老婦人發誓說，她年輕時曾在大約四十年前的意大利見過他，而他那時看起來一模一樣——一個五十歲左右的人。可以想像人們對這個自稱 Saint Germain 伯爵的神秘陌生人有多困惑。

It is no wonder that the King, Louis XV, heard rumours of this stranger
and became curious to meet him, and so the Count of Saint Germain was
invited to the Court, to the Palace of Versailles. The King was greatly
impressed by Saint Germain, by the dignity and wisdom with which he
spoke and by the great knowledge he showed in science, in history, in
every subject one could talk about.

難怪 Louis 十五國王聽到了關於這個陌生人的傳聞後產生了好奇，想要見他，於是 Saint Germain 伯爵被邀請到宮廷——Versailles 宮。國王對 Saint Germain 印象深刻，為他言談中的尊嚴和智慧所折服，也為他在科學、歷史以及任何話題上展現的淵博知識所震撼。

One day King Louis showed Saint Germain a large diamond which
was, however, not very valuable because it had a flaw, a dark spot in it.
Saint Germain said: "Let me have it, I will return it to you without flaw,
without the dark spot in a few weeks." And so he did - yet no one knows
how he could remove a dark spot from inside a diamond. No one can do
it today.

有一天 Louis 國王拿給 Saint Germain 看一顆大鑽石，但因為裡面有一個瑕疵——一個暗斑——所以不太值錢。Saint Germain 說：「把它交給我，幾週後我會把它還給你，沒有瑕疵，沒有暗斑。」他真的做到了——但沒有人知道他是如何從鑽石內部去除暗斑的。至今也沒有人能做到。

It is not surprising that the King became very friendly with this mysterious stranger. It is also not surprising that Madame de Pompadour and
other French noblemen became jealous and began to hate this man who
came from nowhere, who was, perhaps, not even of noble blood but was
so much in the King's favour.

國王和這位神秘的陌生人變得非常親近，這不足為奇。Madame de Pompadour 和其他法國貴族開始嫉妒，開始憎恨這個不知從哪裡冒出來的人——他甚至可能不是貴族血統，卻如此受國王寵幸——這也不令人意外。

And then came a day when the Count of Saint Germain spoke alone to
the King about France and said: "This war with Prussia will ruin France if it
goes on for another few years. And if there is hunger and poverty in the
land, it may well lead to a revolution, to bloodshed and civil war. If you
want to avoid this, Your Majesty, you must end the war with Prussia right
now."

後來有一天，Saint Germain 伯爵單獨對國王談論法國的局勢，說：「如果這場與 Prussia 的戰爭再持續幾年，法國將被摧毀。如果國內出現飢餓和貧困，很可能會導致革命、流血和內戰。陛下，如果您想避免這一切，就必須立刻結束與 Prussia 的戰爭。」

King Louis sighed and said: "Madame de Pompadour and her warminister are quite determined to go on with the war, they are certain we
are going to win. And, frankly, I have not the strength of will to start a
row and a long argument with her and the minister. I have left the worries of government to her and I can't quite suddenly change this arrangement which, after all, saves me all the bother.

Louis 國王嘆了口氣說：「Madame de Pompadour 和她的戰爭大臣非常堅決地要繼續打仗，他們確信我們會贏。而且坦白說，我沒有意志力去和她以及大臣吵一架、進行漫長的爭論。我已經把治國的煩惱都交給了她，我不能突然改變這個安排——畢竟它省去了我所有的麻煩。」

And then the Count of Saint Germain said: "If you dare not openly go
against the wishes of Madame de Pompadour, let me go secretly in your
name and try to make peace with Prussia. Once I have a peace-treaty,
Madame de Pompadour and her minister will not object any longer, and,
perhaps, they will realize that this is the best for all concerned."

然後 Saint Germain 伯爵說：「如果陛下不敢公開違背 Madame de Pompadour 的意願，讓我以您的名義秘密前往，嘗試與 Prussia 締結和平。一旦我拿到了和約，Madame de Pompadour 和她的大臣就不會再反對了，也許他們會意識到這對所有人都是最好的結果。」

Well, the King agreed half-heartedly and gave the Count of Saint Germain permission to go in secret to Holland which was a neutral country
and meet the Prussian ambassador there and discuss a peace-treaty between Prussia and France with him.

國王半心半意地同意了，允許 Saint Germain 伯爵秘密前往中立國 Holland，在那裡會見 Prussia 大使，與他商討 Prussia 和法國之間的和約。

If this plan of the Count of Saint Germain had succeeded, the history
of France, the history of whole Europe would have been different. But
things did not go according to plan.

如果 Saint Germain 伯爵的計劃成功了，法國的歷史、整個歐洲的歷史都會不同。但事情沒有按計劃進行。

Madame de Pompadour and the War Minister had spies who told
them that there were strange goings-on in Holland between Saint Germain and the Prussian
ambassador. Madame de Pompadour went to
Louis, told him she knew he was doing something behind her back and
demanded an explanation. When Louis told her of his secret arrangement with Saint Germain, she was furious. "France is going to win the
war, there is no reason to ask Prussia for peace, and France must not let
down her allies - Russia and the Austrian Empire. The whole stupid plan
had to be stopped right away."

Madame de Pompadour 和戰爭大臣有間諜，間諜告訴他們在 Holland 的 Saint Germain 和 Prussia 大使之間有不尋常的往來。Madame de Pompadour 去找 Louis，告訴他她知道他在背後搞事情，並要求解釋。當 Louis 告訴她他與 Saint Germain 的秘密安排時，她暴怒了。「法國將贏得這場戰爭，沒有理由向 Prussia 求和，法國絕不能辜負她的盟友——俄羅斯和 Austria 帝國。這整個愚蠢的計劃必須立刻停止。」

And Louis, weak as he was, gave in. A message was sent to Holland
that the Count of Saint Germain had no permission and no authority
from the King of France to discuss a peace-treaty and that was the end of
the discussions. The war went on for another four years and ended with
France defeated and poorer then ever before.

而 Louis 像往常一樣軟弱地屈服了。一封訊息被送到 Holland，說 Saint Germain 伯爵沒有法國國王的許可和授權來討論和約——和談就此結束。戰爭又持續了四年，以法國戰敗、比以往更加貧窮而告終。

The Count of Saint Germain disappeared as suddenly as he had come
upon the scene. Some people met him in London a year later, and then

Saint Germain 伯爵消失得和他出現時一樣突然。一年後有人在 London 見到過他，然後

Chapter 23
Rousseau, Voltaire and the Aristocrats

第二十三章
Rousseau、Voltaire 與貴族階級

The story of the Count of Saint Germain shows how the secret brotherhood of the Rosicrucians tried to guide the new age that had begun
with the age of discovery, so that all the changes this new age would
bring should come as blessings and not through chaos and disasters.
But this secret brotherhood had no soldiers, no power to enforce its
wishes, it could only work where there was good will - and in the case of
the Count of Saint Germain the attempt to help France failed.

Saint Germain 伯爵的故事展示了 Rosicrucian 秘密兄弟會如何試圖引導這個始於大發現時代的新紀元，使這個新時代帶來的所有變革能以祝福而非混亂和災難的方式降臨。但這個秘密兄弟會沒有軍隊，沒有強制執行其意願的力量，它只能在有善意的地方運作——而在 Saint Germain 伯爵的案例中，幫助法國的嘗試失敗了。

But there were other ways in which the secret brotherhood could
work. For instance, it could try to bring into the world new ideas. The old
ideas about the divine rights of kings were doomed to go, in some countries sooner, in other countries later, but they would go. If the old ideas
went, however, new ideas had to come. At times this secret brotherhood
worked to bring new ideas into the world.

但秘密兄弟會還有其他的運作方式。例如，它可以嘗試把新的思想帶入世界。關於君權神授的舊觀念注定要消亡，在某些國家快些，在另一些國家慢些，但它們終將消亡。如果舊觀念消失了，新思想就必須到來。有時候這個秘密兄弟會就致力於把新思想帶入世界。

There was a young man, a writer, whose name was Jean Jacques
Rousseau (1712-78). He later became famous, but at that time he was
still unknown. Now the French Academy in Paris - a college of the most
learned men of France, announced a competition for the best essay on
the "Blessings of Civilization." The competitors were supposed to write
about the terrible kind of life amongst the so-called "savages", or in ancient times when our own ancestors were primitive people who lived in
caves, and then the essay should describe how much better people lived
when civilization came, houses, cities, inventions and orderly government.

有一個年輕的作家，名叫 Jean Jacques Rousseau（1712-78）。他後來變得很有名，但當時還默默無聞。當時 Paris 的法蘭西學院——法國最有學問的人組成的學術機構——宣布了一場徵文比賽，題目是關於「文明的祝福」的最佳論文。參賽者應該描寫所謂「野蠻人」中間或古代我們祖先住在洞穴裡的原始生活有多麼可怕，然後描述文明到來後——房屋、城市、發明和有序的政府——人們的生活改善了多少。

Of course, many writers all over France were keen to take part in this
competition; not only was there a sum of money as reward for the best
essay, it would also be a great honour to win the first prize. Jean Jacques
Rousseau also wanted to write an essay in the hope to win the prize. He
was giving much thought to what he should write and how he should
write it - and he used to go for long walks to collect his thoughts. Like
many people he could think better if he went for a walk. On one of these
walks he sat down on a bench and a stranger, a man whom he had never
seen before, sat down beside him and started a conversation.

當然，全法國的許多作家都渴望參加這場比賽；不僅有獎金作為最佳論文的獎勵，贏得頭獎本身也是莫大的榮譽。Jean Jacques Rousseau 也想寫一篇論文，希望能得獎。他苦思冥想該寫什麼、該怎麼寫——他習慣去散長步來整理思緒。像許多人一樣，他散步時思考得更好。在某次散步中，他在一張長椅上坐下，一個他從未見過的陌生人在他旁邊坐下，開始了一段對話。

During the course of this talk, Rousseau told the man that he was
working on this essay on the "Blessings of Civilization."

在這次交談中，Rousseau 告訴那個人他正在寫這篇關於「文明的祝福」的論文。

"Well," said the stranger, "I think it would be much more original if
you were to write the opposite. Show in your essay, how unnatural, how
artificial life has become through civilization - and that human happiness
does not come through civilization but from living in accordance with nature. Is not the beauty of a mountain, of a sunrise, more wonderful than
all the rules and regulations which we call civilization? If you really knew
the so-called "savages" you might find more dignity, more wisdom and
more happiness amongst them than in the so-called civilized nations.
Think about it."

「嗯，」陌生人說，「我認為如果你寫相反的觀點會更加新穎。在你的論文中展示，文明使生活變得多麼不自然、多麼矯揉造作——而人類的幸福不是來自文明，而是來自與自然和諧共處。一座山的美、一次日出的美，難道不比我們所謂文明的所有規則和法規更加美妙嗎？如果你真正了解所謂的『野蠻人』，你可能會發現他們之中有比所謂文明國家更多的尊嚴、更多的智慧和更多的幸福。好好想想吧。」

And with these words the stranger left. Rousseau never saw him
again, but the ideas of the stranger were like seeds which took root in
his own mind. He never wrote the essay on the "Blessings of Civilization,"
but he wrote many books in which he praised the beauty of nature when
it is unspoiled by man.

說完這些話，陌生人離開了。Rousseau 再也沒見過他，但這位陌生人的思想就像種子一樣在他心中生根發芽。他從未寫成那篇「文明的祝福」論文，但他寫了許多書，讚美未被人類破壞的大自然之美。

Before Rousseau no one had ever written about climbing mountains
for pleasure or enjoying a walk in a forest. It was through the books of
Rousseau that the beauty of nature was popularized.

在 Rousseau 之前，從來沒有人寫過為了樂趣而攀登山嶺或享受林間漫步的文章。正是透過 Rousseau 的著作，大自然之美才被推廣開來。

But Rousseau also wrote about government. Is it natural for men to
have kings, to obey the laws of kings? No, answered Rousseau - man is
born with natural rights which no king can take from him. Kings and royalty and noblemen with special privileges and rights are not natural -
and what is not natural is wrong.

但 Rousseau 也寫了關於政府的著作。人類擁有國王、服從國王的法律是自然的嗎？不，Rousseau 回答——人生來就有任何國王都不能剝奪的天賦權利。國王、王室和擁有特殊特權的貴族都不是自然的——而不自然的就是錯的。

The books of Rousseau became famous, they were read by thousands
in France and soon in other countries of Europe and in America. These
books opened people's eyes, and they began to think about government
as they had never thought before.

Rousseau 的著作聲名遠播，在法國有成千上萬人閱讀，很快傳到歐洲其他國家和美洲。這些書打開了人們的眼睛，他們開始以前所未有的方式思考政府。

It all began with that stranger who met Rousseau on his walk. But this
meeting was no accident. The stranger was a member of that secret
brotherhood that wanted to guide people to new ideas.

這一切始於那個在散步時遇見 Rousseau 的陌生人。但這次相遇並非偶然。那位陌生人是秘密兄弟會的成員，他們想要引導人們走向新思想。

But Rousseau was not the only writer of that time who made people
think. Another Frenchman of that time was Voltaire (1694-1778). Voltaire
was a man of great wit who showed what was wrong with France in his
books, what was wrong with its government and wrong with its rich,
spoiled noblemen - by making fun of it all.

但 Rousseau 並不是那個時代唯一啟發人們思考的作家。那個時代的另一位法國人是 Voltaire（1694-1778）。Voltaire 是一個才智過人的人，他在書中揭露法國的弊病、政府的問題、富有而被慣壞的貴族的毛病——用嘲諷的方式。

People laughed when they read Voltaire's books, but they laughed at
things they had formerly respected: the authority of the King, the
privileges of the noblemen, the
power of the Catholic Church. They
laughed - and as they laughed, they lost their respect for those in authority. Unfortunately, in his books Voltaire made fun of religion altogether, not only of the Church and wicked priests.

人們讀 Voltaire 的書時會笑，但他們笑的是他們以前尊敬的東西：國王的權威、貴族的特權、天主教會的權力。他們笑著——笑著笑著，就失去了對當權者的敬畏。不幸的是，Voltaire 在書中嘲弄的不僅是教會和邪惡的神父，而是整個宗教。

And through his books many people lost their faith in God altogether
and became enemies of religion.

透過他的書，許多人完全失去了對上帝的信仰，成為宗教的敵人。

The books of Voltaire, like those of Rousseau, influenced and changed
the ideas of millions of people all over Europe, but especially in France.
People looked upon the Church, the King, the noblemen with new eyes;
the old authority was gone and people saw their rulers and masters in a
new light, the light of reason. That's why this time is called the period of
Enlightenment. And in this enlightenment - in the light of reason - the
King and his courtiers and noblemen did not look as if they deserved the
respect which they had enjoyed before.

Voltaire 的著作，和 Rousseau 的一樣，影響並改變了全歐洲數百萬人的思想，尤其是在法國。人們用新的眼光看待教會、國王和貴族；舊的權威消失了，人們在新的光芒——理性之光——中審視他們的統治者和主人。這就是為什麼這個時期被稱為啟蒙時代。而在這啟蒙之光——理性之光——的照耀下，國王和他的朝臣、貴族看起來並不配享有他們以前所享有的尊敬。

Let us try to get a picture of life in France at that time.

讓我們試著描繪那個時代法國的生活圖景。

The nobility - or as they are called, the aristocrats - the dukes, counts,
barons, earls, lived a life of ease and pleasure; they owned great estates
on which the peasants worked and toiled to provide their noble masters
with the money they needed to live in style.

貴族——或者被稱為「貴族階級」（aristocrats）——公爵、伯爵、男爵、勳爵，過著安逸享樂的生活；他們擁有大片莊園，農民在上面辛勤勞作，為他們的貴族主人提供過上體面生活所需的金錢。

It was quite unthinkable that an aristocrat ever did any physical work
or went into business. An aristocrat could become a courtier of the King,
or even a minister, and many of them became officers in the army. It
would have been a disgrace for an aristocrat to own a shop or to become a carpenter or even to be a doctor though. There was, however,
no need for the aristocrats to enter any of the despised professions as
their estates provided the money they needed. And, to make life even
sweeter for them, the aristocrats did not have to pay any taxes. It was
the common people who paid the taxes which kept the royal court in
luxury. No wonder these aristocrats, lords and ladies, had a lot of time
and money to spend on their clothes.

一個貴族做體力勞動或經商是完全不可想像的。貴族可以成為國王的朝臣，甚至成為大臣，他們中的許多人成為軍隊裡的軍官。然而，一個貴族如果開店或當木匠甚至當醫生，那將是奇恥大辱。不過，貴族們也沒有必要從事任何這些被看不起的職業，因為他們的莊園已經提供了他們所需的金錢。而且讓他們的生活更加甜美的是，貴族不需要繳任何稅。是平民百姓繳的稅維持著王室宮廷的奢華。難怪這些貴族男女有大量的時間和金錢花在穿著上。

By this time, from about 1700 onwards, men no longer wore wigs
with curly hair. They still wore wigs, but the wigs had rolls on either side
and ended at the back in a pig-tail decorated with a large black bow.
This wig was no longer in any natural colour but was powdered white.
People's houses had special powder cabinets where servants used
clouds of flour to whiten their masters' wigs.

到了這個時候，大約從 1700 年起，男人不再戴捲髮假髮了。他們仍然戴假髮，但假髮兩側有卷筒，後面以一條裝飾著大黑蝴蝶結的辮子收尾。這種假髮不再是任何自然的顏色，而是撲了白粉的。人們的房子裡有專門的撲粉室，僕人在裡面用大量的麵粉把主人的假髮撲白。

The clothes men wore were colourful: blue, fawn, pink, and there was
fine lace worn at the neck and on the wrists. A three-cornered hat, and a
thin sword, completed a gentleman's dress.

男人穿的衣服色彩繽紛：藍色、淡黃褐色、粉色，脖子和手腕處佩戴精美的蕾絲。一頂三角帽和一把細劍，就構成了紳士的完整裝扮。

Ladies' dress was even more elaborate. A lady's hair-do was piled up
high on the head and so complicated that a woman had to spend more
than an hour every morning - with the help of several maids - to get her
hair right. The skirts were enormous, bulging out widely from the hips by
means of a wire-structure underneath.

女士的服裝更加華麗繁複。女士的髮型高高盤在頭上，如此複雜以至於每天早上要花一個多小時——在幾位女僕的幫助下——才能把頭髮弄好。裙子巨大無比，靠下面的金屬裙撐從臀部向外大幅膨脹。

Both, ladies and gentlemen, took pride in graceful manners and
graceful movements. A man's bow, a woman's curtsey, were practised
and practised endlessly to make them beautiful.
Every gesture and every movement was the result of endless practice.
To talk, sit down or stand up naturally as we do, would have seemed to
these people crude and barbaric. Even taking "snuff" (which was then a
great fashion) and blowing his nose afterwards, was something that had
to be done gracefully.

男女貴族都以優雅的舉止和動作為傲。男人的鞠躬、女人的屈膝禮，都要反覆練習再練習以求完美。每一個手勢和動作都是無盡練習的結果。像我們這樣自然地說話、坐下或站起來，在這些人看來會顯得粗魯和野蠻。即使是吸鼻煙（當時非常流行）以及之後擤鼻子，都必須做得優雅得體。

But the men who took so much trouble over their clothes and graceful manners were not weaklings. They were tough and they did not wear
their thin swords (foils) for nothing. The slightest provocation - a real or
imaginary insult - and a duel followed

但這些在穿著和優雅舉止上如此講究的男人並不是軟弱之輩。他們很強悍，身上佩戴的細劍可不是裝飾。只要有最輕微的挑釁——一個真實的或想像中的侮辱——接下來就是決鬥。

It was a great life for the aristocrats, but not for the greater part of
the population. It was a miserable life for the peasant who had no rights
at all, not even the right to leave the service of his master. He belonged
to the farm like the trees that grew on it. They lived in hovels, together
with pigs and goats and chickens. They wore tattered rags and they had
no education whatsoever.

對貴族來說這是美好的生活，但對大部分人口來說並非如此。對農民來說，這是悲慘的生活，他們完全沒有權利，甚至沒有離開主人的權利。他和農場上生長的樹木一樣屬於這片農場。他們住在茅棚裡，和豬、山羊、雞擠在一起。他們穿著破爛的衣裳，完全沒有接受過任何教育。

But, between the aristocrats and the miserable peasants there was a
middle-class, the burghers, or bourgeois. This middle-class, the bourgeois, wanted to change this shameful system.

但在貴族和悲慘的農民之間，有一個中產階級，即市民，或稱布爾喬亞。這個中產階級——布爾喬亞——想要改變這個可恥的制度。

Chapter 24
The American War of Independence

第二十四章
美國獨立戰爭

We looked at the three "classes" into which France was divided at that
time: the nobility or aristocrats, who had rights and privileges - even the
privilege of not paying any taxes; the peasants who had neither rights
nor privileges and lived in poverty and ignorance; and between these
two classes the middle-class or bourgeoisie. The bourgeois were mainly
concentrated in the cities; they were the artisans, carpenters, tailors, the
shopkeepers and merchants, the doctors and lawyers.

我們看了當時法國分成的三個「階級」：貴族或稱貴族階級，他們擁有權利和特權——甚至享有不繳任何稅的特權；農民既沒有權利也沒有特權，生活在貧困和無知之中；而在這兩個階級之間是中產階級或布爾喬亞。布爾喬亞主要集中在城市裡；他們是工匠、木匠、裁縫、店主和商人、醫生和律師。

On the one hand, these bourgeois copied the manners and fashions
of the aristocrats; although the bourgeois were heavily taxed, they also
made money in their trades and professions and they used their money
to dress and live like the aristocrats whom they envied.

一方面，這些布爾喬亞模仿貴族的舉止和時尚；雖然他們被課以重稅，但他們也從自己的行業和職業中賺到了錢，並用這些錢來穿著和生活得像他們羨慕的貴族一樣。

But the bourgeois were also educated people. It was the bourgeois
who read the books of Rousseau and Voltaire and who learned from
these books to regard the whole class-system of France as something
wicked and stupid. Amongst the middle-class, amongst the bourgeois,
the books of the "enlightenment" became like gun-powder needing only
a spark to set off an explosion.

但布爾喬亞也是有教養的人。正是布爾喬亞閱讀了 Rousseau 和 Voltaire 的著作，並從這些書中學會把法國的整個階級制度視為邪惡而愚蠢的東西。在中產階級中，在布爾喬亞中，「啟蒙」的書籍就像火藥一樣，只需一個火花就能引發爆炸。

However, the first explosion did not come in France, it came in far-off
North America where the theories of Rousseau and Voltaire were put
into practice for the first time.

然而，第一次爆炸並非發生在法國，而是發生在遙遠的北美洲，Rousseau 和 Voltaire 的理論在那裡第一次被付諸實踐。

In the Seven Years' War, Britain had fought on the side of Prussia
against France. And the English colonists in America had come faithfully
to the aid of the "old
country" and had fought against the French
colonists in Canada. But those British colonists in America were poorly
rewarded for their loyalty.

在七年戰爭中，英國站在 Prussia 一邊對抗法國。而美洲的英國殖民者忠心耿耿地來支援「母國」，與加拿大的法國殖民者作戰。但這些美洲的英國殖民者忠誠所得到的回報卻很微薄。

The English general who came over to America to command the
colonists showed quite openly his contempt for the rough "colonials" as
he called them. There was one colonial officer, George Washington, who
fought gallantly and bravely, developing a shrewd knowledge of warfare.
Although he had wanted to become a professional soldier, when the
Seven Years' War was over, he left the army because he was disgusted
with the way the men from England had looked down on him and the
other colonials. George Washington left the army and devoted himself to
managing a large farm he had in Virginia.

來到美洲指揮殖民者的英國將軍毫不掩飾地蔑視這些他所謂的粗魯「殖民地人」。有一位殖民地軍官 George Washington 英勇善戰，在戰爭中培養了對戰術的精明理解。儘管他曾想成為職業軍人，但七年戰爭結束後他離開了軍隊，因為他對英格蘭人看不起他和其他殖民地人的方式感到厭惡。George Washington 離開軍隊，專心經營他在 Virginia 的一座大農場。

But the contempt of the English for the American colonials showed itself also in other ways. The Seven Years' War had cost a lot of money and
the Government Treasury was empty, so the King of England, George III,
and the British Government decided that the only way to fill it up again,
was by raising the taxes.

但英國人對美洲殖民者的蔑視也表現在其他方面。七年戰爭花了大量的錢，國庫空虛，於是英格蘭國王 George 三世和英國政府決定，唯一的辦法就是增稅。

That worked in Britain; the people in Britain paid the new taxes dutifully - after all, it was their own Members of Parliament, the Government
they had elected, that had decided these taxes were necessary. But the
colonials in America did not feel like that at all. The American colonials
had no Member of Parliament in London, they were not represented.
And they complained. They complained that there should be "no taxation without representation." This meant, "If we have no say in the Government, we will not pay taxes imposed by the Government."

這在英國行得通；英國人民盡職盡責地繳了新稅——畢竟，是他們自己選出的國會議員、他們自己選出的政府，決定了這些稅收是必要的。但美洲的殖民者完全不是這麼想的。美洲殖民者在 London 沒有國會議員，他們沒有被代表。他們提出了抗議。他們抗議「無代表，不納稅」。這意味著：「如果我們在政府中沒有發言權，我們就不會繳政府強加的稅。」

The Americans sent one of their best men to England to plead for
their rights. His name was Benjamin Franklin. Franklin came from a poor
family, most of what he had learned he had taught himself. When he
read a book, he first read a few pages, then he closed the book, and
wrote down what he remembered, and then he compared his writing
with the book to see if he had left out anything. In this way he had made
himself a famous writer and a man greatly respected by his fellowAmericans. He was also interested in science, experimented with electricity and invented the lightning conductor. This was the man the Americans sent to London to speak for them. But neither George III nor the
ministers of the English Government had much patience for the American complaints and Benjamin Franklin had to go back to America without having achieved anything.

美國人派出了他們最傑出的人之一前往英格蘭為他們的權利申訴。他的名字叫 Benjamin Franklin。Franklin 出身貧寒，他學到的大部分知識都是自學的。他讀書的方法是先讀幾頁，然後合上書，寫下他記得的內容，再把自己寫的和書對照看有沒有遺漏。用這種方法他把自己造就成了一位著名的作家和深受美國同胞尊敬的人。他也對科學有興趣，做過電學實驗並發明了避雷針。這就是美國人派去 London 為他們發聲的人。但 George 三世和英國政府的大臣們對美國人的抱怨都沒有什麼耐心，Benjamin Franklin 不得不空手而歸。

And soon after his return something happened to make things worse
between the colonials and England.

他回去後不久，發生了一件事使殖民者和英格蘭之間的關係更加惡化。

The colonials - who were, after all English and Scots - liked to drink
tea. And when the London Government put a tax on their tea (again
without asking if the colonials agreed) the Americans were very angry.
The tax itself was not much, but it was a matter of principle.

殖民者們——畢竟是英格蘭人和蘇格蘭人——喜歡喝茶。當 London 政府對他們的茶葉課稅時（又是沒有徵求殖民者的同意），美國人非常憤怒。稅本身不多，但這是原則問題。

The Government wanted to show the colonials that they had to pay if
London said so, whether they agreed or not. And the Americans wanted
to show that they were not willing to pay one penny unless they had
agreed to do so. It was a matter of principle on both sides.

政府想要向殖民者表明，只要 London 說了就得繳，不管他們同不同意。而美國人想要表明，除非他們同意，否則一分錢都不願意繳。雙方都認為這是原則問題。

And so, one day, in 1773, three ships carrying cases of tea arrived in
the American port of Boston. But before the cases could be unloaded a
gang of young men, some disguised as Indigenous Americans, boarded
the ships and threw all the tea-cases into the sea. The Americans call this
event the "Boston Tea-Party." It was no more than a prank, but a prank
that led to war.

於是，1773 年的某一天，三艘載著茶葉箱的船抵達美國港口 Boston。但在箱子被卸下之前，一群年輕人——有些人偽裝成原住民——登上了船，把所有的茶葉箱都扔進了大海。美國人把這個事件稱為「Boston 茶黨事件」。這不過是一場惡作劇，但卻是一場導致戰爭的惡作劇。

The English Government sent troops to America to frighten the Americans into obedience. But this only had the opposite effect, it roused the
colonists to fight for their rights.

英國政府派軍隊到美洲，企圖嚇唬美國人服從。但這只產生了相反的效果，反而激起了殖民者為自己的權利而戰。

The Americans called a great meeting in Philadelphia - and at this
meeting or Congress they proclaimed the ideas of Rousseau, the natural
rights of man. The answer of the British Government was to send yet
more soldiers to America. But now the Americans took to arms and at
the small town of Lexington in Massachussets, there came the first battle
which, to their great surprise the English "Redcoats" lost.

美國人在 Philadelphia 召開了一次盛大的會議——在這次會議或大陸會議上，他們宣告了 Rousseau 的思想、人的天賦權利。英國政府的回應是向美洲派出更多士兵。但現在美國人拿起了武器，在 Massachusetts 的小鎮 Lexington，爆發了第一場戰鬥——令英國「紅衫軍」大感意外的是，他們輸了。

Soon the fighting spread, and the Americans elected George Washington as their general and leader the man who had proved his gallantry
and knowledge of warfare.

戰鬥很快蔓延開來，美國人選舉 George Washington 為他們的將軍和領袖——這個在戰場上證明了自己勇氣和軍事才能的人。

It was a sad time when English-speaking people fought each other in
America. And, as always, other nations came in on either side. German
troops came to America to fight for the British, and French volunteers
led by a nobleman, Lafayette, came to fight on the side of the Americans.

說英語的人在美洲互相廝殺，這是一段悲傷的歲月。而且像往常一樣，其他國家也分別加入了兩方。德國軍隊來到美洲為英國而戰，由貴族 Lafayette 率領的法國志願者則來到美國人一邊作戰。

As the war progressed, the Americans decided that they wanted more
than just fair treatment from the London Government, they wanted
complete independence with their own government in America.

隨著戰爭的推進，美國人決定他們要的不僅僅是 London 政府的公平對待，他們要的是完全的獨立，在美洲建立自己的政府。

On the July 4, 1776 the delegates meeting in Philadelphia proclaimed
the British colony to be the independent United States of America. July 4
is still celebrated in America as Independence Day, a national holiday to
commemorate the famous Declaration of Independence. The new country was not going to have a king, it was not to have any noblemen and
aristocrats, it was a land where all men should have equal rights, the natural rights of man. They would elect in freedom their own government
and president.

1776 年 7 月 4 日，在 Philadelphia 開會的代表宣告英國殖民地成為獨立的美利堅合眾國。7 月 4 日至今仍在美國作為獨立日來慶祝，這是紀念著名的《獨立宣言》的國定假日。這個新國家不會有國王，不會有貴族和貴族階級，這是一片所有人都應享有平等權利——人的天賦權利——的土地。他們將自由地選舉自己的政府和總統。

In the American Declaration of Independence, the ideas of the Enlightenment, the ideas of Rousseau and Voltaire, were put into practice
for the first time.

在美國的《獨立宣言》中，啟蒙運動的思想、Rousseau 和 Voltaire 的思想，第一次被付諸實踐。

George III and the British Government were not at all pleased to lose
the American colony, and they continued the war against the "colonials."
But General Washington had made a fighting force of his untrained
rough colonials that could beat the trained and drilled British Redcoats.
The French volunteers under Lafayette also fought gallantly, and the
British could not win against people who fought for their freedom.

George 三世和英國政府對失去美洲殖民地非常不滿，他們繼續對「殖民地人」作戰。但 Washington 將軍把他那些未經訓練的粗獷殖民者打造成了一支能夠擊敗訓練有素的英國紅衫軍的戰鬥力量。Lafayette 率領的法國志願者也英勇奮戰，英國人無法戰勝為自由而戰的人民。

Moreover, in Britain, there were a good many people - even amongst
members of the Government - who were in sympathy with the Americans. And amongst the common people of England there was little
enthusiasm for a war against colonists who were English and many of
whom still had relatives in England. And so, after eight years the War of
Independence came to an end. In 1783 peace was made, and it was Benjamin Franklin who signed the peace-treaty in which Britain recognized
her former colony as an independent "sovereign" state. And it was
George Washington who became the first president of the new country,
the United States of America.

而且在英國，有相當多的人——甚至在政府成員中——同情美國人。在英格蘭的普通百姓中，對一場與殖民者的戰爭——這些殖民者是英格蘭人，其中許多人在英格蘭還有親戚——也缺乏熱情。於是，經過八年，獨立戰爭走向了終結。1783 年雙方締結了和約，正是 Benjamin Franklin 簽署了那份英國承認其前殖民地為獨立「主權」國家的和約。而成為這個新國家——美利堅合眾國——第一任總統的，正是 George Washington。

In the American War of Independence the ideas of human rights had
won their first victory.

在美國獨立戰爭中，人權的理念贏得了它的第一場勝利。

Chapter 25
Louis XVI. The Three Estates

第二十五章
Louis 十六與三級會議

In the history of ancient times one hears mainly about kings and noblemen, and even the crusades were still lead and commanded by noble
knights and kings. Joan of Arc was, perhaps, the first commoner to lead
armies and to change the course of history. But then, with the beginning
of the new age, the great men came from the middle-classes; Leonardo,
Columbus, Galileo. But the middle-classes could not only produce artists,
explorers, scientists, they could also produce statesmen and generals, as
Cromwell had shown in England, and as Washington and Franklin
showed in America a century later. You can see that we have come to the
time when the middle-class, the "bourgeois" became the people who
made history. And the American War of Independence was a victory for
"bourgeois," for middle-class people who had taken up arms to fight for
their rights.

在古代歷史中，人們主要聽到的是國王和貴族的故事，甚至十字軍也仍然由貴族騎士和國王率領和指揮。Joan of Arc 或許是第一個率領軍隊並改變歷史進程的平民。但隨後，隨著新時代的開始，偉人開始來自中產階級：Leonardo、Columbus、Galileo。中產階級不僅能產生藝術家、探險家、科學家，也能產生政治家和將軍，正如 Cromwell 在英格蘭所展示的，以及一個世紀後 Washington 和 Franklin 在美洲所展示的。你可以看到我們已經來到了中產階級——「布爾喬亞」——成為創造歷史之人的時代。而美國獨立戰爭就是「布爾喬亞」的勝利，是拿起武器為自己權利而戰的中產階級人民的勝利。

In 1774 while this War of Independence was still in progress, Louis XV
died in France (Madame de Pompadour had died before him). But few
Frenchmen had any tears for a king who had remained spoiled all his life
without a thought to his responsibilities as ruler of the nation. Louis' son
had died before him, so it was his grandson, Louis XVI of France, who became King at the age of nineteen.

1774 年，當獨立戰爭仍在進行時，Louis 十五在法國去世了（Madame de Pompadour 比他先走）。但很少有法國人為一位終其一生被慣壞、從不考慮他作為國家統治者的責任的國王流淚。Louis 的兒子先他而去，所以是他的孫子——法國的 Louis 十六——在十九歲時登上了王位。

Young Louis XVI was not madly ambitious for glory, like Louis XIV, le
Roi Soleil, nor did he care for senseless amusements like Louis XV, he was
a much better man who took his duties as King of France very seriously.
But France was by then in a terrible plight and only a man of great genius might have found a way to solve the urgent problems of the country. But Louis XVI was not a genius, he was not even clever, and the only
thing he was really good at was his hobby: making iron-locks. In his
spare-time he hammered happily in a little workshop rigged up for him
at Versailles - but this innocent hobby was no help in solving the problems of France.

年輕的 Louis 十六不像太陽王 Louis 十四那樣瘋狂地追求榮耀，也不像 Louis 十五那樣沉迷於無謂的享樂。他是一個好得多的人，非常認真地對待他作為法國國王的職責。但法國此時已處於可怕的困境，只有一個天才或許才能找到解決國家迫切問題的方法。然而 Louis 十六不是天才，他甚至算不上聰明，他唯一真正擅長的是他的嗜好：製造鐵鎖。業餘時間他會在 Versailles 為他搭建的一間小工作坊裡快樂地敲敲打打——但這個無害的嗜好對解決法國的問題毫無幫助。

Nor was his wife - whom he loved dearly - a great help. Queen Marie
Antoinette was an Austrian princess, the daughter of Maria Theresa. She
was lively, liked fine clothes and gay parties, but she was not clever.
Marie Antoinette,
too, had a hobby. You see, by this time Rousseau's
